This preliminary geotechnical exploration report presents the results of our 
limited field exploration and laboratory testing performed for the Kaneohe to Kailua 
Wastewater Facilities Plan project from the Kaneohe Wastewater Treatment Plant to the 
Kailua Regional Wastewater Treatment Plant in the District of Koolaupoko on the Island 
of Oahu, Hawaii. 
Our work was performed in general accordance with the scope of services 
outlined in our fee proposals of August 13, 2008 and April 19, 2010. 
Please note that the soil and core samples recovered during our field exploration 
(remaining after testing) will be stored for a period of two months from the date of this 
preliminary geotechnical exploration report. The samples will be discarded after that 
date unless arrangements are made for a longer storage period. Please contact our 
office for alternative sample storage requirements, if appropriate. 
If you have questions or need additional information, please contact our office.

SECTION 1.  PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
An updated comprehensive wastewater facilities plan will be developed for the 
Island of Oahu. An integral part of this plan is the construction of a deep, large diameter 
tunnel to convey wastewater between the facilities. One of the initial tasks consisted of 
establishment of a geotechnical testing plan for a preliminary geotechnical engineering 
exploration for the tunnel. This task consisted of research of readily available existing 
boring data from government and private files. Based on the subsurface information 
obtained as well as other pertinent data related to the proposed tunnel construction, a 
geotechnical testing plan, as reference above, was developed. 
The proposed tunnel storage/conveyance system is comprised of various sizes. 
The portion of the tunnel system from the new Kailua Regional Wastewater Treatment 
Plant (RWWTP) Influent Pump Station to the Kaneohe Preliminary Treatment Facility New 
Drop Structure is planned at 10.5 and 13 feet (inside diameter). The invert elevations of 
the tunnel system along this stretch range from about -60 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL) to 
about -45 feet MSL (from Kailua to Kaneohe). The ground surface elevations along this 
portion of the system range from about +10 to +60 feet MSL with an average of about 
+30 feet MSL. The borings will need to extend approximately two tunnel diameters (about 
30 feet) below the invert elevations. Based on this information, the average depth of the 
borings will be approximately 130 feet below the ground level. 
We understand a portion of this alignment from Kailua to Kaneohe extends offshore 
and below Kaneohe Bay. For this preliminary geotechnical engineering exploration, we do 
not plan to perform any over water borings at this time. 
For the portion of the tunnel system from the Kaneohe Preliminary Treatment 
Facility New Drop Structure to the Ahuimanu Preliminary Treatment Facility New Drop 
Structure, the tunnel diameter is planned at 7 feet (inside diameter). The invert 
elevations of the tunnel system along this stretch range from about -45 feet MSL to 
about -22 feet MSL (from Kaneohe to Ahuimanu). The ground surface elevations along 
this portion of the system range from about +10 to +100 feet MSL with an average of 
about +60 feet MSL. In one area of the tunnel alignment, the ground surface elevations 
PGER 07/15/2011

SECTION 2.  SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program was to obtain 
an overview of the surface and subsurface conditions to develop a soil and/or rock data 
set in support of the preliminary feasibility study for the Kaneohe to Kailua Wastewater 
Facilities Plan project. Our work was performed in general accordance with the scope of 
services outlined in our fee proposals dated August 13, 2008 and April 19, 2010. In 
order to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and laboratory testing 
program consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Preparation of a Health and Safety Plan specific to the work being 
performed by Geolabs personnel and our subcontractors for the project. 
2. 
Application for the necessary excavation permits from applicable 
government agencies for boring locations within the public right-of-way. 
3. 
Coordination of underground utility clearances with various utility companies 
and toning of the boring locations by Geolabs and its subcontractors. 
4. 
Preparation of traffic control plans and provision of signs, cones, and 
police officers for traffic control during the field exploration program. 
5. 
Mobilization and demobilization of truck-mounted drill rigs, water trucks, and 
operators to the proposed boring locations. 
6. 
Drilling and sampling of 22 borings, extending to depths of about 110.5 to 
320.5 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface, totaling about 
3,009 lineal feet of exploration. 
7. 
Performance of Seismic Shear Wave Velocity tests in 16 selected borings to 
determine the shear wave velocity profile of the subsurface materials.  
8. 
Conversion of five selected borings to groundwater monitoring wells using 
2-inch ID, Schedule 40 PVC slotted and riser pipes with sand pack/grout 
backfill and traffic rated covers.  
9. 
Monitoring of the groundwater levels in the groundwater monitoring wells 
utilizing dataloggers. 
10. 
Performance of 21 packer injection tests where coral or basalt rock 
formation is encountered at the tunnel depths.  
11. 
Performance of constant head and falling head permeability tests in 
19 selected borings (before and after soil sampling) at the proposed tunnel 
depths.

Performance of 106 point load tests on selected rock cores retrieved at

SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu generally is composed of the weathered and dissected 
remnants of two extinct shield (domed) volcanoes – Waianae and Koolau. The older 
Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western third of Oahu’s landmass while the 
younger Koolau Volcano forms a major portion of the eastern two-thirds of Oahu’s 
landmass. Generally, it is believed that the Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes 
began erupting on the order of about 20 to 25 million years ago as deep ocean 
submarine volcanoes. Subaerial volcanic eruptions on the Island of Oahu ended 
approximately 2.2 to 3.5 million years ago in the Pleistocene Epoch. Furthermore, the 
Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano remained active; therefore 
the eastern flank of Waianae Volcano is partially overlapped and buried below the 
younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior portions of the Island of Oahu. 
In addition to the primary volcanic composition of the Island of Oahu, other 
secondary and generally more localized geologic earth materials comprise the lowland, 
coastal, and valley portions of the island. The secondary earth materials include various 
alluvial and colluvial deposits, oceanic and marine sediments, and submerged and 
emerged coralline reef materials. 
The geology of the Kaneohe Bay area is highly complicated and related to the 
formation of the Island of Oahu in its current form. The Island of Oahu was built by the 
extrusion of basalt and basaltic lavas from the Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes. 
The older Waianae Volcano is estimated to be middle to late Pliocene in age, and the 
younger Koolau Volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to early Pleistocene in age. 
Shield volcanoes form at the intersection of two or more rift zones, or lines of 
weakness in the earth’s crust. During the active phase of a volcano, magma from the 
underlying magma chamber is injected along the rift zones with the highest frequency of 
injection occurring near the intersection of the rifts. Magma that reaches the ground 
surface will create a vent or eruptive center, which allows lava to flow at the surface. 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 7 
Hawaii ● California 
The injected magma is usually in a sheet-like form, rather than a tubular pipe; 
and, when the magma cools, it forms a wall-like structure of dense rock referred to as a 
dike. In the central portion of the volcano, dikes will be so frequent that they will form a 
complex with often intertwining, overlapping or intersecting dikes. As the volcano 
matured, the repeated withdrawal of magma from the chamber results in subsidence of 
the rocks above the chamber. This subsidence results in a large summit crater, or 
caldera. 
The caldera of the Koolau Volcano extends from Kaneohe to Waimanalo and 
from the Koolau Pali to seaward beyond Lanikai. The project site is within the Koolau 
Caldera Complex. The original rocks in the complex generally consisted of nearly level 
bedded caldera filling basalts. Much of the rock was intruded by dikes during volcanic 
activity, and portions were chemically altered by hydrothermal activity within the caldera 
complex. In some areas, the chemical alteration softened the rocks into an easily 
eroded material. 
The Koolau Pali is a long escarpment extending from Makapuu at the southern 
end of the island to Punaluu in the north. Conventional wisdom has been that the Pali 
was formed by the coalescence of amphitheater headed valleys in the more erodible 
rocks of the Koolau Caldera Complex. However, there are more recent theories that 
postulate that the eastern portion of the Koolau Volcano faulted and slumped off 
exposing the caldera complex to the forces of erosion. It is most likely that the Pali is the 
result of a combination of both faulting and erosion. 
After a long period of volcanic inactivity, during which time erosion incised deep 
valleys into the Koolau Shield, volcanic activity returned with a series of lava flows 
followed by cinder and tuff cone formations. These series are referred to as the 
Honolulu Volcanic Series. 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (Ice Age), sea levels fluctuated in response to the 
cycles of continental glaciation. As the glaciers grew and advanced, less water was 
available to fill the oceanic basins such that sea levels fell below the present stands of 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 8 
Hawaii ● California 
the sea. When the glaciers melted and receded, an excess of water became available 
such that the sea levels rose to above the present sea level. 
The processes of erosion and deposition were affected by these glacio-eustatic 
sea level fluctuations. When the sea level was low, the erosional base level was 
correspondingly lower, and valleys were carved to depths below the present sea level. 
When the sea level was high, the erosional base level was raised such that sediments 
accumulated at higher elevations. 
In the mountainous regions of Hawaii, the erosional processes are dominated by 
detachment of soil and rock masses from the valley walls and are transported 
downslope toward the axis of a valley, primarily by gravity, as colluvium. Once these 
materials reach the stream in the central portion of a valley, alluvial processes become 
dominant, and the sediments are transported and deposited as alluvium. 
In general, stream flows in Hawaii are intermittent and flashy, such that the 
stream flows transmit large volumes of water for very short durations. Because of this, 
the transport of sediments is intermittent, and the bulk of the stream’s hydraulic load 
consists of a poorly sorted mixture of boulders, cobbles, gravel, sands, and fines. When 
the erosional base levels change, these sediment loads are left as deposits. 
When deposits are left in place for long periods of time, chemical processes 
begin to alter the materials simultaneously causing a breakdown or weathering of the 
material. Chemical processes also cause induration or cementation of the 
coarse-grained portion of the sediment into a poorly consolidated sedimentary rock, or 
conglomerate. Simultaneously, erosion continues in the areas above the valley floors 
and upstream in headwaters. This continued erosion generates material that is 
transported downslope covering the older alluvial deposits. 
Depending on the local base level and rate of transport, these newer sediments 
are generally transient in terms of geologic time. In addition, their consistency and 
density are generally less than those of the older, partially consolidated deposits. The 
deposits of low-permeability soil horizons at the base of the Koolau Range have created 
a confined aquifer condition. At the Kaneohe area, these confined aquifers are supplied 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii ● California 
by groundwater from dikes within the Koolau Mountain Range. Because water within a 
confined aquifer is under pressure, boreholes that penetrate these aquifers will 
encounter a piezometric or artesian head of groundwater. 
The following paragraphs provide a generalized description of the major 
processes that contributed to the geomorphic evolution of the Island of Oahu. 
The Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes formed during the late Pliocene and 
early Pleistocene Epochs by the sequential deposition of basaltic lava flows. As 
mentioned previously, the main shield-building activity ceased about 2.2 to 3.5 million 
years ago. Evidence from previous deep drilled wells indicates that the Island of Oahu 
has subsided by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of this early volcanic activity 
(Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During that period of slow island subsidence, coral-algal reefs grew and 
fossilized along the southern coast of the Island of Oahu. As a result, broad 
embayments formed that were bounded by fringing barrier reefs whose growth 
essentially kept pace with the slow rate of island subsidence. Thereafter, a series of 
shallow lagoons formed behind the barrier reefs and both terrigenous and marine 
sediments accumulated within the lagoons (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (most recent Ice Age), many sea-level fluctuations 
occurred because of the waxing and waning of global continental glaciation. Generally 
speaking, as the great continental glaciers accumulated, the global level of the oceans 
fell because there was less water available to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the 
glaciers receded (melted), the global level of the oceans rose because of the water 
influx. The landmass elevation of the Island of Oahu remained essentially stable during 
these sea-level changes; therefore, the fluctuations were eustatic in nature. The 
glacio-eustatic fluctuations resulted in stands of the sea that were either higher or lower 
relative to the present sea-level conditions. The following are some generalized 
examples of the geomorphic effects that resulted from the changes in sea level relative 
to the island landmass. 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii ● California 
The higher sea-level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and depositional 
fans composed of terrigenous sediment at the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea stands caused accumulation of coralline reef materials at correspondingly 
higher elevations and the deposition of lagoonal and marine sediments in the quiet 
backwaters protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level stands caused streams to incise erosional valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef platforms. In addition, subaerial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardening of the calcareous reef materials. Finally, 
renewed subaerial erosion (acting on the upper regions of the volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energy 
depositional environments, which is typified by a poorly sorted mixture of alluvial 
materials with a greater range in particle sizes. 
During periods of no significant sea level change, continued stream flow 
extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the deposition of alluvium 
overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed six main stratigraphic units within the Kaneohe to Kailua 
Wastewater Facilities Plan area. The stratigraphic units consist of soil and rock 
materials of varying character and engineering properties. The six main stratigraphic 
units are listed below in the general order of geologic age, beginning with the oldest unit 
and ending with the youngest unit: 
Koolau Basalt (Tkkb) 
Koolau Dike Complex (Tkdc) 
Koolau Volcanic Breccia (Tkbr) 
Honolulu Volcanic Series (Qhb) 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii ● California 
Units that may be found in the boring logs are locally used as descriptive terms, 
but they may not necessarily conform to the main stratigraphic units listed above, such 
as the following: 
Coralline Deposits 
Fill (limited in thickness and variable) 
The following subsections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of 
the above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, past experience with 
similar materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at 
selected locations along the project corridor. The approximate delineation of these 
stratigraphic units at the ground surface along the segment alignment is presented on 
the Geology Map, Plate 4. 
3.2.1 Koolau Basalt (Tkkb) 
This consists of the flank and caldera flows of the original Koolau Volcano. Flank 
flows tend to be thinly bedded flows of tholeiitec basalt in both the pahoehoe and 
a`a forms. Caldera filling flows tend to be more thickly bedded and more massive in 
structure. Included in this unit are areas that were mapped previously as members 
of the Kailua Volcanic Series. It may have a mantle of cobbles and boulders, or 
residual soils, overlying the top of the basalt rock formation. In addition, it is 
anticipated that various types of volcanic rock sub-components, such as clinker 
layers and lava tubes, also may be encountered within the basalt rock formation. 
Structurally, Koolau Basalt and its subunits are among the strongest of all the units 
encountered within the project corridor. The following subunits of the Koolau Basalt 
are used in this preliminary geotechnical exploration report: 
3.2.1.a Residual Soil 
Residual soils are completely weathered volcanic rocks that have lost all 
visible rock texture characteristics. The residual soils at the project site are 
typically brown to reddish brown in color. The residual soils may be 
mottled with various colors. Residual soils are comprised mainly of clayey 
materials with stiff to very stiff consistency. Typically, residual soils occur 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii ● California 
overlying saprolitic soils or weathered basalt formation. It should be noted 
that relict boulders of hard rock may be encountered in residual soils. In 
addition, there is potential for encountering hard basalt rock ledges in 
residual soils because the volcanic rock may not have weathered 
uniformly throughout the rock formation. Some relict boulders of hard rock 
occasionally may be encountered in residual soils. 
3.2.1.b Saprolite 
Extremely weathered volcanic rock is referred to as saprolitic soils (or 
saprolites). Saprolitic soils represent rock that has been reduced by 
weathering to soil-like components with weathered rock fragments. The 
soil material retains the remnant rock texture such as layering, 
vesicularity, and some fracture patterns. Saprolitic soils are typical of the 
tropical weathering of volcanic rocks and are composed of mainly silty 
materials. The coloring scheme of saprolitic soils depends on the type of 
parent volcanic rock from which the soils are derived and the degree of 
chemical weathering that the material has experienced. Because the 
parent material does not weather uniformly throughout the formation, relict 
boulders of hard rock and/or hard rock ledges occasionally may be 
encountered within saprolitic soils. 
3.2.1.c Clinker 
Clinker is the fragmental basalt found on the top, and sometimes at the 
bottom, of a’a lava flows. Clinker generally is gravel to cobble-sized 
fragments of scoriaceous rock in a tightly packed but open-graded 
structure. 
3.2.1.d Basalt Formation 
This subunit is used to designate the in-situ lava formation. This subunit 
occurs as red, brown, or gray, severely fractured to massive, medium hard 
to very hard rock formation. 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii ● California 
3.2.2 Koolau Dike Complex (Tkdc) 
This unit consists of areas of Koolau Basalt that have been intensely intruded by 
volcanic dikes. Hydrothermal action during the active phase of Koolau Volcano 
has altered some of the country rock (or host rock) into weaker materials and 
altered the chemical properties of the other portions of the country rock. The 
dikes themselves form walls of dense strong rock varying in thickness from a few 
inches to tens of feet. These walls are generally impermeable and intersecting 
dikes will form subterranean compartments that will perch groundwater at high 
elevations. 
3.2.3 Koolau Volcanic Breccia (Tkbr) 
This unit consists of brecciated, or broken, angular rock fragments, which may, or 
may not, be consolidated into a rock material. Within the project area, these 
breccias may be either explosion breccias resulting from explosive events during 
the eruptive phase of Koolau Volcano or the later post-erosional Honolulu 
Volcanic Series; or, they may be fault related resulting from subsidence of the 
Koolau Caldera. 
3.2.4 Honolulu Volcanic Series (Qhb) 
After the eruptive phase of Koolau Volcano ended, there was a long period of 
erosion, and then, about one million years ago, volcanic activity was renewed 
with a series a smaller short duration eruptive events in the southeastern part of 
the Island of Oahu. These events were generally somewhat explosive in nature 
due to interaction between the magma and groundwater, and the deposits were 
usually tuff or cinder cones. 
3.2.5 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older Alluvium consists of alluvial soil materials deposited during the very late 
Pleistocene and very early Holocene Epochs as a result of erosion in higher 
areas. These materials range from fine to coarse grained and are well 
consolidated. In some cases, the materials have indurated to the point of being a 
poorly consolidated sedimentary rock and then further weathered to a residual 
type soil. 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii ● California 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of time 
by more recent deposits to become somewhat consolidated, and then subsequently 
uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic processes. This unit 
generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of terrigenous sediments, which 
have been transported by stream action from the upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channel sometimes are encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
may resemble saprolite soil deposits but are clearly differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit high strength, which leads to a relatively 
high bearing or load carrying capacity with generally low settlement characteristics. 
Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell potential when 
subjected to fluctuations in moisture content.  
3.2.6 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated sediments, which 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. Recent alluvium 
generally is found within some stream channels and bordering some stream banks, 
at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors and basin features 
surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in proximity to swamp and tidal 
flat environments. 
The soils assigned to this unit generally are considered poorly to very poorly suited 
for use as support for loads, due to their low in-situ strength characteristics and the 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ● California 
tendency for excessive settlement to occur in structures overlying soils of this unit. 
The following subunits are used in this preliminary geotechnical exploration report. 
3.2.6.a Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
3.2.6.b Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
3.2.6.c Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with 
fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized 
by a moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile 
mollusk shells. 
3.2.6.d Estuarine Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brownish gray silty sand 
or sandy silts with some organic materials in a soft or loose consistency. 
This soil unit originates in shallow estuary conditions where the discharge 
of fresh water from streams and drainage courses introduces terrigenous 
sediments that mix with lagoonal or littoral sediments.  Generally, 
estuarine deposits lack the characteristic organic odor of marsh or 
lagoonal deposits. 
3.2.7 Coralline Deposits 
Being in a subtropical region, Hawaii does not have the extensive coral reefs as 
found in more tropical regions.  Reefs here are built of a combination of corals 
and calcareous algae, and the growth pattern is discontinuous.  Coral reef 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ● California 
limestone usually is encountered only on the forereef areas or where an emerged 
reef has become consolidated or cemented through chemical weathering.  
Locally, the term “coralline deposits” is used to describe reef materials that 
appear to be in their original growth or deposition position. 
Wave action and biological activity can break coralline deposits into clastic 
materials.  When these materials are encountered, usually as a sandy gravel or 
gravelly sand, they are referred to as “coralline detritus,” because the deposit is 
largely detrital fragments of coral and calcareous alga. 
3.2.8 Fill 
Fill is used in boring logs and other graphic representations to differentiate man-
made deposits from natural deposits.  The material is heterogeneous and does 
not necessarily represent an engineered fill.  The soil material composition is 
variable, ranging from clays to boulders, and the consistencies of the materials 
are variable, ranging from soft and/or loose to very hard and/or very dense. 
3.2.9 Groundwater 
Groundwater typically occurs in Hawaii as one of four types of conditions:  Basal 
groundwater table, basal lens, perched groundwater, or dike impounded 
groundwater. Basal groundwater tables occur where a saturated zone develops in 
response to the local base elevation.  
A basal lens will develop when the fresh water level is confined by salt water in 
accordance with the Ghyben-Herzberg Principle, which is based on observations 
that fresh groundwater floats on saline groundwater in coastal regions. The ratio of 
densities between fresh and salt water with salinity compared to that of seawater, 
causes the salt water to be displaced downward about 40 feet below sea level for 
every foot that the fresh basal water table or potentiometric head rises above sea 
level. 
Perched groundwater develops where vadose water encounters a confining, or 
impermeable, layer above the local base elevation. This impermeable layer retards 
the percolation of the vadose water forming a localized groundwater body. These 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 3.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ● California 
perched water bodies may be temporary, in response to climatic conditions, or 
permanent if there is sufficient recharge. 
Dike impounded groundwater occurs with the dike complexes of the Hawaiian 
volcanoes where generally impermeable and intersecting dikes will form 
subterranean compartments, which will perch groundwater at high elevations. 
Within the project area, basal groundwater lenses are not well developed due to 
the low permeability of the surface materials and the lack of recharge area. When 
groundwater is encountered, it will be most likely basal groundwater table, 
perched, or dike impounded. It should be noted that, for tunneling projects, dike 
impoundment can develop very high hydrostatic pressures that can present 
hazards for tunneling equipment and personnel. 
For this project, groundwater was encountered in the borings at depths of about 
25 feet below the existing ground surface at the time of our field exploration. The 
groundwater levels measured in the borings generally correspond to between about 
Elevations -21 and +30 feet MSL. Considering the proximity of the project site to the 
Pacific Ocean, groundwater levels are expected to vary with tidal fluctuations, 
seasonal rainfall, and time of the year.

SECTION 4.  GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
The preliminary geotechnical exploration program conducted for the project 
generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work components. A brief 
description of each work component is presented below. Detailed discussions, findings, 
and the test results of the various components are provided in the respective 
appendices herein. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in 
the introduction to Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the 
methods and procedures. 
Typically, the soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch diameter 
continuous-flight solid-stem augers and continued with PQ-size wireline coring methods 
to advance the PQ size casing. Sampling of the soils was performed using the modified 
California sampler (ASTM D 3550) and the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split spoon 
sampler (ASTM D 1586). In the softer soils, relatively undisturbed samples were 
obtained by pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled steel Shelby tube into the soil formation 
(ASTM D 1587).  
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil 
formations, samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel also were obtained 
and retained in core boxes for visual observation. In some cases, the borehole was 
advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a rotary wash drilling method; therefore, there 
were no soil samples retrieved between the soil sampling points (soil sampling by the 
modified California sampler or the split spoon sampler for the Standard Penetration 
Test). The borings were advanced into the rock formations with the PQ-size wireline 
coring methods. The PQ size wireline coring methods produce rock core samples that 
are 3.35 inches in diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586. SPT sampling provides a measure of 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 4.  GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii ● California 
is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured 
as described in Appendix A. ASTM D 6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining 
Rock Quality Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ 
with core diameters of 36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally 
acceptable for measuring RQD as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do 
not cause excessive core breakage or poor recovery, or both. The NX-size (54.7 mm 
[2.16 in.]) and NQ-size (47.5 mm [1.87 in.]) are the optimal core sizes for measuring 
RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters provided the core diameter is 
clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in Appendix G for information 
and reference. 
During the field exploration program, our geologists performed pocket 
penetrometer tests on selected cohesive soil samples. Pocket penetrometer tests 
provide an indication of the unconfined compressive strength of a soil sample. The 
pocket penetrometer test values are shown on the Logs of Borings in Appendix A. 
4.2 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at 16 of the completed 
boreholes, using seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) equipment. The test 
procedures are discussed in more detail in the introduction to Appendix B.  
In brief, the completed borehole was tremie-filled with coated bentonite pellets 
(below groundwater) in order to provide a medium through which the seismic cone tip 
could be pushed. The sleeve and tip resistance through the bentonite-filled borehole are 
not meaningful because the cone is pushed through the bentonite backfill. The seismic 
cone tip contains a geophone sensor. At a succession of depths, the advancement of 
the cone tip was halted, a seismic pulse was generated at the ground surface by striking 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 4.  GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 20 
Hawaii ● California 
a loaded plank with a switched hammer, and the resulting seismic shear wave was 
detected at the geophone. The distance from the source and the wave arrival time allow 
for determination of the average shear wave velocity over a depth interval. The 
measured average seismic shear wave velocity logs are presented in Appendix B. 
4.3 
In-Situ Permeability Testing 
Packer injection tests were performed in selected coral and basalt formation 
intervals encountered in the borings. The packer test provides the in-situ transmissivity 
(permeability) of the coral and basalt formation to evaluate the tunnel and shaft 
groundwater control requirements. The test results are presented in Appendix C. 
In-situ percolation tests using constant head and falling head methods with well 
point-filter were conducted at selected depths in some of the borings. The percolation 
tests provide the approximate in-situ coefficient of permeability. The test results also are 
presented in Appendix C. 
4.4 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
Vibrating wire piezometers were installed in five selected test borings to monitor 
the piezometric water levels and their seasonal fluctuations. The water level data was 
recorded with a datalogger from the time of installation through December 2010. Details 
of the piezometer installation and the piezometric level readings are included in 
Appendix D. 
4.5 
Point Load Testing 
Point load tests were performed on selected rock cores obtained during our field 
exploration program in general accordance with ASTM D 5731 – Determination of the 
Point Load Strength Index of Rock and Application to Rock Strength Classifications. 
Generally, point load tests are index strength tests for rock materials. Point Load 
Strength Index (Is(50)) were determined from 106 diametral and axial tests performed on 
selected rock cores retrieved at Boring Nos. 2, 5, 6, 7, 12, 13, 17 and 22 during our field 
exploration program. Tests with fracture surfaces passing through only one loading 
point were rejected and were not reported. The point load test results are summarized 
on Plates E-1 through E-3 in Appendix E.  
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 4.  GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii ● California 
4.6 
Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory tests were performed on selected soil and rock core samples to 
determine the physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included 
moisture content, unit weight, Atterberg limits, grain size distribution, consolidation, 
unconsolidated-undrained (UU) triaxial compression, unconfined compressive strength 
of rock, ring swell and corrosivity tests (pH, minimum resistivity, chloride content, and 
sulfate content).  The laboratory test results are summarized on the boring logs and 
presented in Appendix F. 
4.7 
Special Rock Testing 
Special rock tests were performed by the Earth Mechanics Institute at the 
Colorado School of Mines on selected rock core samples recovered during our 
preliminary field explorations. The special rock tests performed included indirect 
(Brazilian) tensile strength, Cerchar Abrasivity Index, punch penetration test, and 
petrographic analysis. Details of the special rock tests conducted, and the test results 
are provided in Appendix H.

SECTION 5.  SUMMARY OF PHYSICAL AND ENGINEERING PROPERTIES

Kaneohe to Kailua Wastewater Facilities Plan area. The stratigraphic units consist of 
soil and rock materials of varying character and engineering properties. The following 
sections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the stratigraphic units 
based on our field and laboratory tests conducted for selected soil and rock core 
samples obtained from the borings along the project corridor.

encountered basalt formation in Boring Nos. 2, 4, 6, 16, 21 and 22. The following table

5.10 Lagoonal Deposit and Estuarine Deposit 
Lagoonal Deposits and Estuarine Deposits tend to be under-consolidated and 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. In general, lagoonal

SECTION 6.  LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this preliminary 
geotechnical exploration report are based upon information obtained from the field 
exploration points. Variations of subsurface conditions between and beyond the 
exploration points may occur, and the nature and extent of these variations may not 
become evident until additional field investigation and/or construction is underway. 
The locations of the field exploration points indicated in this report have been 
estimated and measured from the ground features shown on Google Earth maps. 
Elevations of the field exploration points were estimated from the Topographic Base 
Map downloaded from Wilson Okamoto Corporation’s ftp site on January 28, 2010.  The 
locations and elevations of the field borings should be considered accurate only to the 
degree implied by the methods used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of 
borings in this report were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times 
shown on the graphic representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of Wilson Okamoto 
Corporation and their client, the City and County of Honolulu, Department of 
Environmental Services, for specific application to the Kaneohe to Kailua Wastewater 
Facilities Plan project in accordance with generally accepted geotechnical engineering 
principles and practices.  No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This preliminary geotechnical exploration report presents information on the 
general subsurface conditions for the planning and evaluation of the proposed tunnel 
storage/conveyance system. This preliminary geotechnical exploration report is limited 
in scope and is for the project design advancement and cost estimation purposes only. 
Therefore, this report may not contain sufficient data, or the proper information, to serve 
as a basis for final design and construction cost estimates. Final design of the tunnel 
PGER 07/15/2011
SECTION 6.  LIMITATIONS  
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 29 
Hawaii ● California 
project will require the performance of additional site-specific exploration for detailed 
design.  
This preliminary geotechnical exploration report was not intended to investigate 
the potential for presence of hazardous materials existing at the project site. It should be 
noted that the equipment, techniques, and personnel used to conduct a 
geo-environmental exploration differ substantially from those applied in geotechnical 
engineering.

A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions along the Kaneohe to Kailua Wastewater 
Facilities Plan project by drilling and sampling 22 borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 
through 22 (B-1 through B-22). The borings extended to depths ranging from about 
110.5 to 320.5 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface using various 
truck-mounted drill rigs equipped with continuous-flight augers and rotary coring tools. 
The field exploration was conducted from September 8, 2009 through June 4, 2010. 
The boring locations are shown on the Overall Site Plan (Plate 2) and Site Plans 
(Plates 3.1 through 3.7). 
Soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch ID continuous-flight 
solid-stem augers, and continued with PQ-size wireline coring method to advance 
PQ size casing. After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the 
boreholes, the bored holes (with the exception of those being converted to groundwater 
monitoring points) were generally backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite 
pellets (below the groundwater level) and bentonite chips (above the groundwater 
level). The temporary casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to 
place the bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and advancing upward. The top 
3 feet of the borehole was then grouted with Quikcrete concrete mix. No additives were 
added to the drilling water during our drilling operations. 
Our geologists classified the materials encountered in the borings by visual and 
textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488, Standard 
Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling operations 
on a near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed visually and by 
testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with ASTM D 2487, 
Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil 
Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as 
groundwater levels, etc. are presented on the Logs of Borings, Plates A-1 through A-22. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the borings drilled in 
general accordance with ASTM D 3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained 
from the borings drilled in general accordance with ASTM D 1586, Standard Test 
Method for Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD 
standard penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts 
needed to drive the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are 
shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths.  Relatively “undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were 
obtained by pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil 
PGER 07/15/2011
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JULY 2011     Page A-2 
Hawaii ● California 
formation in general accordance with ASTM D 1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled 
Tube Sampling of Soils for Geotechnical Purposes. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the soil sample. Results of the pocket penetrometer 
tests are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system 
with double tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2113, Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation.  Core drilling is a rotary 
drilling method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left 
in the hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. 
Rock cores were described in general accordance with the Rock Description System, as 
shown on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.2.  The Rock Description System is based on 
the publication “Suggested Methods for the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in 
Rock Masses” by International Society for Rock Mechanics (March 1977).  
Recovery (REC) may be used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the 
relative quality of rock masses, where appropriate. Recovery is defined as the actual 
length of material recovered from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. 
For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery 
would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run in rock that is 
sound material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting 
any drilling, mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound 
material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and 
would be shown on the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used 
to describe the relative quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical 
Properties of Rocks and Minerals” by Robert S. Carmichael (1989).

The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite

A P P E N D I X   B 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling of the subsurface materials was performed 
using Seismic Piezocene Cone Penetration Testing (SCPTU) equipment. Shear wave 
velocity testing using seismic piezocone penetration testing equipment was performed 
at 16 selected boring locations, designated as B-2 through B-4, B-7 through B-16, and 
B-18 through B-20. 
The seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at approximately 6-foot 
intervals extending to depths ranging from about 59 to 139.5 feet below the existing 
ground/pavement surface. In order to conduct the shear wave velocity test in the 
borings, the test borings generally were advanced utilizing rotary coring methods to the 
maximum depth of the borings. Logs of the materials encountered in the borings are 
presented in the Logs of Borings in Appendix A. After the borings were advanced to the 
maximum depth of the boreholes, the bored holes were backfilled with 0.25-inch 
diameter coated bentonite pellets. The temporary casing from the coring operations was 
used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and 
advancing upward. When the bentonite pellets are in contact with the groundwater in 
the borehole, the pellets started to hydrate slowly. As the bentonite pellets hydrate, they 
swell and soften. The probes were then pushed through the softened bentonite using 
the seismic piezocone penetration testing equipment (SCPTU). 
The seismic shear wave velocity test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton 
steel electronic subtraction cone into the bentonite backfilled borehole. The cone has an 
apex angle of 60 degrees and a projected surface area of 1.55 square inches 
(10 square centimeters) equipped with an electronic depth sensor attached to the cone 
rod. The seismic cone carries a uniaxial horizontal geophone to detect the arrival of a 
shear wave generated and propagated from the ground surface. Seismic measurements 
are made when the SCPT has stopped and a shear wave is sent into the subsurface. A 
shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a loaded plank with a switched 
hammer. The propagation time of the wave from the hammer blow to the cone was 
measured at each discrete depth interval. The vector difference of these depths divided 
by the time difference for the shear wave to arrive at the various depths provided the 
average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The shear wave velocities measured at the boring locations are presented on 
Plates B-1 through B-16 in Appendix B.

GEOLABS, INC. 
JULY 2011     Page C-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   C 
In-Situ Permeability Testing 
In-situ permeability tests using a PQ Wire Line Packer System Type II were 
performed in 17 borings as described below. Packer injection tests provide in-situ 
transmissivity (permeability) of formations with various degrees of fracture and 
weathering conditions. Nineteen packer injection tests were conducted at selected coral 
and basalt rock intervals encountered in the borings to characterize the hydraulic 
properties of coral and basalt rocks. 
The packer injection test was performed after completion of drilling to the desired 
depth. An inflatable rubber packer (single-packer) was lowered to the testing depth to 
the bottom of the borehole. The testing interval then was sealed by the pressurized 
rubber packer at the top of the interval. 
The packer tests were conducted in general accordance with ASTM D 4630 – 
Standard Test Method for Determining Transmissivity and Storage Coefficient of 
Low-Permeability Rocks by In Situ Measurements Using the Constant Head Injection 
Test.  The water inflow quantity versus time was measured until steady-state flow was 
reached.  Details of the packer testing apparatus are shown on Plate C-1. The packer 
test results are presented on Plates C-2 through C-22. 
Twenty-two in-situ percolation tests using constant head or falling head methods 
were performed in 19 boring locations. The in-situ percolation tests provide the 
coefficient of permeability within the test interval. 
The constant head tests were performed by pumping water into the boreholes 
until the water surface was stabilized and remained at equilibrium for a sufficient period 
of time.  The water height and the rate of pumping were then measured to provide data 
upon which to base calculation of the subsoil permeability. 
During the falling head testing, the boreholes were carefully filled with water to 
the top of the boreholes.  The time intervals and water drops were then recorded to 
provide data upon which to base calculation of the permeability values of the subsoils.  
The permeability test results are presented on Plates C-23 through C-44.

JULY 2011     Page D-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   D 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled 
for the project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and 
summarized in Table D-1. In addition, groundwater monitoring points were established at 
five boring locations, including Boring Nos. B-12, B-8, B-12, B-15 and B-20, to monitor 
seasonal fluctuations and to measure groundwater gradients along the project. 
To convert to the groundwater monitoring wells, the boreholes at the above 
locations were backfilled with bentonite to depths of about 20 feet below the groundwater 
level. Two-inch diameter Schedule 40 PVC pipe consisting of screen and solid riser for 
the remaining depth were then placed into the boreholes. The screen was embedded in 
the filter sand with 12 inches of cushion below the screen and 12 inches capping above 
the screen.  The remaining riser was surrounded by bentonite backfill until about 3 feet 
below the ground surface. A 12-inch diameter well cover with traffic rated plate was 
installed for the access of groundwater level monitoring. Details of the typical groundwater 
monitoring well are present on Plate D-1. 
A total of five vibrating wire piezometers, Model 4500 manufactured by Geokon, 
Inc., were installed at selected depths within the borings to monitor the groundwater 
levels on a near-continuous basis using a Model LC-2 datalogger manufactured by 
Geokon, Inc. Prior to the installation, the filter stone was saturated by placing the 
piezometer under water a minimum of 12 hours and transported to the field in a 
submerged condition. In addition, zero readings were obtained in the field prior to the 
installation of each piezometer, which provided a baseline reference under field 
conditions and served as an acceptance test – a verification that the piezometer is 
working properly. The piezometers are embedded in a filter sand pack and sealed at the 
top and bottom with bentonite to isolate the piezometer at the specific strata where 
groundwater level measurements are desired. 
The groundwater level data were downloaded in the field to a laptop computer on 
a regular basis and processed in our office. It should be noted that there are some 
periods where the data acquisition lost some of the data collected resulting in blanks in 
the data set. Typical groundwater level data obtained by the piezometers are shown on 
Table D-2. Graphic presentations of the groundwater level data collected are provided on 
Plates D-2 through D-6. 
PGER 07/15/2011
GEOLABS, INC. 
Hawaii ● California

A P P E N D I X   E 
Point Load Testing 
Point load testing was performed on selected rock cores obtained during our field 
exploration program in general accordance with ASTM D 5731 – Determination of the 
Point Load Strength Index of Rock and Application to Rock Strength Classifications.   
The point load test measures the Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) of rock 
specimens in the form of either core (the diametral and axial tests), cut blocks (the block 
test), or irregular lumps (the irregular lump test). The rock specimens are broken by 
application of concentrated load through a pair of spherically truncated, conical platens.  
For this project, Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) were determined from one hundred 
six (106) diametral and axial tests performed on selected rock cores retrieved at B-2, 
B-5, B-6, B-7, B-12, B-13, B-17 and B-22 during our field exploration program. Tests 
with fracture surface passing through only one loading point were rejected and not 
reported in this appendix. 
The point load tests were performed using a Point Load Test Apparatus 
manufactured by Shambhavi IMPEX. The apparatus consists of a rigid frame, two point 
load platens, a hydraulically activated ram with pressure gauge and a device for 
measuring the distance between the loading points. The pressure gauge is of the type 
in which the failure pressure can be recorded. The test involves compressing a rock 
sample between conical steel plates until failure occurs. The following data were 
recorded from which the Uncorrected Point Load Strength Index (Is) of the specimen was 
determined: 
Is  = P / De
2, MPa 
where: 
P = failure load, N, 
De = equivalent core diameter, mm, and is given by: 
De
2  = D2 for diametral core tests without penetration, mm2 
or 
De
2  = 4A/ for axial, block, and lump tests, mm2 
where: 
A = WD = minimum cross-sectional area of a plane through the platen contact 
points  
The Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) then is obtained by applying a 
“Size Correction Factor F” to the Uncorrected Point Load Strength Index (Is): 
PGER 07/15/2011
Appendix E 
Point Load Testing 
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JULY 2011     Page E-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Is(50) = F * Is  
where: 
F = (De/50)0.45 
The estimated uniaxial compressive strength (c) may be obtained by the 
following formula: 
c = K * Is(D) 
where: 
c  = uniaxial compressive strength, MPa 
K    = index to strength conversion factor that depends on site-specific correlation 
between c and Is(D), MPa 
Is(D) = corrected point load strength index at a given (D) 
The field data collected and the determination of the Point Load Strength Index 
(Is(50)) and the estimated uniaxial compressive strength (c) are presented on Plates E-1 
through E-3.

A P P E N D I X   F 
Laboratory Testing 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the 
Kaneohe to Kailua Wastewater Facilities Plan project were visually examined and 
classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected samples were tested to 
evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties of the soils and rock 
formation encountered. The laboratory testing program was formulated with the 
emphasis on evaluating the moisture, density, plasticity, gradation, compressibility, 
shear strength, and corrosivity of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture content, Atterberg Limits, sieve analysis, consolidation, triaxial compression, 
unconfined compression, ring swell and corrosivity tests. Results of the tests performed 
are summarized in Tables F-1.1 through F-1.15 and are presented on Plates F-1 
through F-55. Detailed descriptions of the various tests performed for this exploration 
are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture content and dry density tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to aid in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and 
correlating the layers between test borings. The moisture content tests were performed 
in general accordance with ASTM Test Designation D 2216. The test results are 
presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Forty-eight (48) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in 
classifying the soils. The Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance 
with ASTM D 4318. The test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentation of the test results are provided on 
Plates F-1 through F-5. 
Sieve Analysis  
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
twenty-six (26) Sieve Analysis tests were performed to evaluate the gradation 
characteristics and to aid in soil classification. The tests were performed in accordance 
with ASTM C 136.  Graphic presentation of the test results are provided on Plates F-6 
through F-11.  
PGER 07/15/2011
Appendix F 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6174-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
JANUARY 2011     Page F-2 
Hawaii ● California 
Consolidation 
Four Consolidation tests were performed on selected soil samples to evaluate 
the compressibility characteristics of the on-site soils. These tests were performed in 
accordance with ASTM D 2435, and the test results are plotted on Plates F-12 through 
F-15. 
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, thirty-seven (37) 
Unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 2850. The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” 
samples. The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates F-16 
through F-52. 
Unconfined Compression – Rock Cores 
Twenty-seven (27) Unconfined Compression tests (ASTM D 7012 Method C) 
were performed on selected rock cores to evaluate their unconfined compressive 
strength of the rock formation encountered. Unconfined compression test results are 
summarized on Plate F-53. 
Ring Swell Test 
Two (2) one-inch Ring Swell tests were performed on relatively undisturbed 
samples to evaluate the swelling potential of the near-surface soils under a small 
surcharge pressure. The test results are summarized on Plate F-54. 
Corrosivity Test 
Fourteen (14) pH (ASTM G51) tests and twelve (12) minimum resistivity 
(ASTM G57) tests were performed on selected soil samples obtained from our field 
exploration by our office. In addition, eleven (11) sets of chloride (EPA 300) and sulfate 
(EPA 300) contents were performed by TestAmerica, Inc. on selected soil samples 
obtained from our field exploration. The test results are presented on Plate F-55.

SUMMARY OF RING SWELL TESTS
F - 54
NOTE:
Relatively Undisturbed
Remolded
Samples tested were either relatively undisturbed or remolded in 2.4-inch diameter by 1-inch high rings.
They were air-dried overnight and then saturated for 24 hours under a surcharge pressure of 55 psf.

Key to Petrographic and Photomicrographic Descriptions
Clay minerals common in altered rocks must often be identified by X-ray diffraction
either because their optic properties are not diagnostic or because they are too fine grained to
be reliably identified by optical methods.  The term "clay" is used herein to denote fine grained
phyllosilicates in general.  Under ideal conditions, it is often possible to optically discriminate
between 4 major groups: kaolinite, smectite, mica (including illite), and chlorite.  This is done
whenever conditions permit.
The term "sericite" is applied to fine grained colorless phyllosilicates that show upper
2nd order maximum interference colors.  These could include muscovite, illite, paragonite,
lepidolite, margarite, clintonite, pyrophyllite, and talc.  The term "intermediate clay" is applied to
fine grained very pale or colorless phyllosilicates that show upper 1st order maximum
interference colors.  These are probably dominated by chlorite, smectite, and mixed-layer
illite/smectite.
The term "opaques" is used to refer to all materials opaque (and sometimes semi-
opaque) to transmitted light.  The term "FEOH" is herein used to indicate fine grained,
yellowish to reddish brown, earthy materials of varying opacity in transmitted light.  FEOH is
probably mostly Fe oxy-hydroxides but may sometimes include sphalerite, realgar, orpiment,
jarosite, a number of Mn oxy-hydroxides, and organic matter.
A question mark after a rock or mineral name in a petrographic description means that
there is uncertainty about the identification of that rock or mineral.
Particle size distributions are given as (A-B ìm), where A and B are the median and
largest particle sizes, respectively, in microns.  A question mark (?) in the position of A or  B
indicates that the value of A or B was indeterminate, probably because of excessively large or
small particle size or statistically insignificant numbers of particles.  
Mineral abundances are visual estimates for an entire slide.  For multi-lithologic
materials (cuttings, etc...), mineralogy, textures, and alteration are described only for the
dominant lithology.
Section preparation codes are as follows: (1) Format: 27 x 46 mm, 51 x 76 mm, or 1"
round; (2) Finish: standard lapping (STD) or polished (POL); (3) Stains: sodium cobaltinitrite
(SCN), alizarin red S (ARS), potassium ferricyanide (PF), and barium chloride + potassium
rhodizonate (BCPR); and (4) Cover: none, permanent Loctite acrylic (PLA), or removable
Canada Balsam (RCB).
Photomicrograph captions/labels contain the following items of information in
consecutive order separated by forward slashes: (1) sample identification; (2) film roll number;
(3) frame number; (4) illumination; (5) field of view (FOV);  and (6) the job identification
number.  "PPL" indicates plane-polarized light; "XPL indicates cross-polarized light;  "R"
indicates reflected light.  "550" means that a 550 nanometer wavelength plate was inserted in
the light path.  "C" indicates that the substage condenser was in (sometimes used for Fe-
oxides).  "O" indicates substage condenser in an oblique position.  These various illuminations
can be combined.  "CON" indicates conoscopic illumination.  POL means that a polarizing filter
was used with the lens, and DAY means the sample was photographed in diffused daylight.
Features on photomicrographs are indicated by the number of the feature in the
ALTERATION section of the text or by a mineral name abbreviation: Quartz, Plagioclase, K-
feldspar, sericite, biotite, ferroan calcite, actinolite.
For hypertext links to images to function properly, the images must reside in the same
folder as the report.

SAMPLE #
B-17 88'
July 14, 2010
ROCK NAME
ALTERED BASALT -- probably formed by alteration (secondary clay + opaques +
K-feldspar + chlorite + quartz) of an aphyric basalt flow or very shallow intrusion.
MINERALS
Plagioclase (20%) + clay (20%) + opaques (20%) + clinopyroxene (10%) + basalt
glass (10%) + K-feldspar (8%) + chlorite (5%) + quartz (2%).
TEXTURES
Aphanitic, hypocrystalline, aphyric, fine grained.
Fabric: non-directed 
Grain Contacts: straight to curved
Microfractures: absent
Phenocrysts (0%) were not observed.
Groundmass (100%) is dominated by plagioclase + clay + opaques + clinopyroxene
+ basalt glass + K-feldspar.
Vesicles (0%) were not observed.
Xenoliths (0%) were not observed.
ALTERATION
The following alteration features are also present but of indeterminate relative ages:
(1) veinlets of quartz + opaques; (2) plagioclase weakly altered to K-feldspar; (3)
clinopyroxene moderately altered to clay; and (4) basalt glass moderately altered to
opaques.
SECTIONING
Format: 27 x 46 mm  Finish: STD  Stains: SCN (top ½) + [ARS + PF] (whole slide) Cover: PLA

B-13 92.5'

ROCK NAME
ALTERED BASALT -- probably formed by cataclasis and alteration (secondary
quartz + FEOH + clay + K-feldspar) of an aphyric basalt flow or very shallow
intrusion.
MINERALS
Plagioclase (45%) + clinopyroxene (31%) + quartz (7%) + FEOH (7%) + opaques
(5%) + clay (3%) + K-feldspar (2%).
TEXTURES
Aphanitic, hypocrystalline, aphyric, fine grained.
Fabric: non-directed 
Grain Contacts: straight to curved
Microfractures: absent
Phenocrysts (0%) were not observed.
Groundmass (100%) is dominated by plagioclase + clinopyroxene.
Vesicles (0%) were not observed.
Xenoliths (0%) were not observed.
ALTERATION
The following alteration features are also present but of indeterminate relative ages:
(1) cataclasis; and (2) cataclastic structures mineralized by quartz.  The following
alteration features are also present but of indeterminate relative ages: (1)
plagioclase weakly altered to clay + K-feldspar; and (2) opaques moderately altered
to FEOH.
SECTIONING
Format: 27 x 46 mm  Finish: STD  Stains: SCN (top ½) + [ARS + PF] (whole slide) Cover: PLA

SAMPLE #
B-5 107'
July 14, 2010
ROCK NAME
ALTERED BASALT BRECCIA -- probably formed by cataclasis and alteration
(secondary clay + opaques + chlorite + quartz + FEOH + K-feldspar) of various 
aphyric basalt flow or very shallow intrusion.
MINERALS
Clay (39%) + plagioclase (25%) + opaques (15%) + clinopyroxene (10%) + chlorite
(5%) + quartz (3%) + FEOH (2%) + K-feldspar (1%).
TEXTURES
Aphanitic, holocrystalline, cataclastically brecciated.
Fabric: non-directed 
Grain Contacts: tangential
Microfractures: absent
Breccia Clasts (ind %) are subround, lithic fragments of various altered aphyric to
sparsely microporphyritic basalt.
Matrix (ind %) is composed of the altered comminuted equivalent of the clasts,
suggesting a dominantly cataclastic mechanism of brecciation.
Cement (5%) is composed of quartz + chlorite.
ALTERATION
The following alteration features are also present but of indeterminate relative ages:
(1) cataclasis; and (2) cataclastic structures mineralized by quartz + chlorite.  The
following alteration features are also present but of indeterminate relative ages: (1)
plagioclase weakly altered to clay + K-feldspar; (2) opaques weakly altered to
FEOH; and (3) clinopyroxene strongly altered to clay + opaques + FEOH.
SECTIONING
Format: 27 x 46 mm  Finish: STD  Stains: SCN (top ½) + [ARS + PF] (whole slide) Cover: PLA

PLATE H-52

3315 NE 112th Ave. #B98  Vancouver, WA 98682-8733  USA  www.petrography.com
SAMPLE #
B6 92.5
July 29, 2010
ROCK NAME
ALTERED BASALT BRECCIA -- probably formed by cataclastic brecciation and
alteration (secondary clay + calcite + quartz) of an aphyric basalt flow protolith.
MINERALS
Plagioclase (50%) + clay (24%) + opaques (10%) + clinopyroxene (8%) + calcite
(8%) + quartz (<1%).
TEXTURES
Aphanitic, holocrystalline, cataclastically brecciated.
Fabric: non-directed
Grain Contacts: tangential
Microfractures: absent
Breccia Clasts (ind %) are subangular, lithic fragments of various altered aphyric
basalt flows.
Matrix (ind %) is composed of the altered comminuted equivalent of the clasts,
suggesting a dominantly cataclastic mechanism of brecciation.
Cement (9%) is composed of calcite ± quartz ± opaques.
ALTERATION
Alteration features in relative chronological order from oldest to youngest are: (1)
cataclasis; and (2) mineralized cataclastic structures + vesicles + breccia cement
composed of calcite ± quartz ± opaques.  The following alteration features are
present but of indeterminate relative ages: (1) clinopyroxene moderately altered to
clay.
SECTIONING
Format: 27 x 46 mm  Finish: STD  Stains: SCN (top ½) + [ARS + PF] (whole slide) Cover: PLA

SAMPLE #
B13 108.5
July 29, 2010
ROCK NAME
ALTERED BASALT BRECCIA -- probably formed by cataclastic brecciation and
alteration (secondary clay + FEOH + calcite + gypsum) of an aphyric basalt flow -
protolith.
MINERALS
Clay (60%) + plagioclase (15%) + clinopyroxene (8%) + opaques (7%) + FEOH
(5%) + calcite (3%) + gypsum (2%).
TEXTURES
Aphanitic, holocrystalline, cataclastically brecciated.
Fabric: non-directed
Grain Contacts: tangential
Microfractures: absent
Breccia Clasts (ind %) are subangular, 800-600 ìm, lithic fragments of various
altered aphyric basalt flows.
Matrix (ind %) is composed of the altered comminuted equivalent of the clasts,
suggesting a dominantly cataclastic mechanism of brecciation.
Cement (9%) is composed of calcite ± quartz ± opaques.
ALTERATION
Alteration features in relative chronological order from oldest to youngest are: (1)
cataclasis; (2) cataclastic structures mineralized by clay; and veins of calcite. The
following alteration features are present but of indeterminate relative ages: (1)
vesicles filled with clay + calcite ± gypsum; (2) clinopyroxene strongly altered to
clay; (3) basalt glass completely altered to clay; (4) plagioclase moderately altered
to clay; and (5) opaques moderately altered to FEOH.
SECTIONING
Format: 27 x 46 mm  Finish: STD  Stains: SCN (top ½) + [ARS + PF] (whole slide) Cover: PLA
